<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="text-align:center;padding-bottom:0;background:#90ee90;font-size:100%;font-weight:bold;">Social implications</div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content plainlist">

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below hlist">
<ul><li><span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tree_of_life.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="760" data-file-width="915" decoding="async" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Tree_of_life.svg/16px-Tree_of_life.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Tree_of_life.svg/24px-Tree_of_life.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Tree_of_life.svg/32px-Tree_of_life.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Evolutionary_biology" title="Portal:Evolutionary biology">Evolutionary biology portal</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="185" data-file-width="180" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/16px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/23px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/31px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png 2x" title="Category" width="16"/> <a href="/wiki/Category:Evolutionary_biology" title="Category:Evolutionary biology">Category</a></span></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Index_of_evolutionary_biology_articles" title="Index of evolutionary biology articles">Related topics</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Evolutionary_biology" title="Template:Evolutionary biology"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Evolutionary_biology" title="Template talk:Evolutionary biology"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Evolutionary_biology&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, <b>taxonomy</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <i> </i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%84%CE%AC%CE%BE%CE%B9%CF%82" title="wikt:τάξις">τάξις</a><i> (<a href="/wiki/Taxis" title="Taxis">taxis</a>)</i> 'arrangement', and <i> </i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%BD%CF%8C%CE%BC%CE%BF%CF%82" title="wikt:νόμος">-νομία</a><i> (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/-nomy" title="wikt:-nomy">-nomia</a>)</i> '<a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">method</a>') is the <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientific</a> study of naming, defining (<a href="/wiki/Circumscription_(taxonomy)" title="Circumscription (taxonomy)">circumscribing</a>) and classifying groups of biological <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> based on shared characteristics. Organisms are grouped into <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxa</a> (singular: taxon) and these groups are given a <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_rank" title="Taxonomic rank">taxonomic rank</a>; groups of a given rank can be aggregated to form a more inclusive group of higher rank, thus creating a taxonomic hierarchy. The principal ranks in modern use are <a href="/wiki/Domain_(biology)" title="Domain (biology)">domain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" title="Kingdom (biology)">kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phylum</a> (<i>division</i> is sometimes used in botany in place of <i>phylum</i>), <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">class</a>, <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a>, <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>. The Swedish botanist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> is regarded as the founder of the current system of taxonomy, as he developed a ranked system known as <a href="/wiki/Linnaean_taxonomy" title="Linnaean taxonomy">Linnaean taxonomy</a> for categorizing organisms and <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">binomial nomenclature</a> for naming organisms.
</p><p>With advances in the theory, data and analytical technology of biological systematics, the Linnaean system has transformed into a system of modern biological classification intended to reflect the <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> relationships among organisms, both living and extinct.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The exact definition of taxonomy varies from source to source, but the core of the discipline remains: the conception, naming, and classification of groups of organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilkins2011_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkins2011-1">[1]</a></sup> As points of reference, recent definitions of taxonomy are presented below:
</p>
<ol><li>Theory and practice of grouping individuals into species, arranging species into larger groups, and giving those groups names, thus producing a classification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Judd_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Judd-2">[2]</a></sup></li>
<li>A field of science (and major component of <a href="/wiki/Systematics" title="Systematics">systematics</a>) that encompasses description, identification, nomenclature, and classification<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simpson_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simpson-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li>The science of classification, in biology the arrangement of organisms into a classification<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></li>
<li>"The science of classification as applied to living organisms, including study of means of formation of species, etc."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li>"The analysis of an organism's characteristics for the purpose of classification"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henderson_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henderson-6">[6]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Systematics studies <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a> to provide a pattern that can be translated into the classification and names of the more inclusive field of taxonomy" (listed as a desirable but unusual definition)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>The varied definitions either place taxonomy as a sub-area of systematics (definition 2), invert that relationship (definition 6), or appear to consider the two terms synonymous. There is some disagreement as to whether <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_nomenclature" title="Biological nomenclature">biological nomenclature</a> is considered a part of taxonomy (definitions 1 and 2), or a part of systematics outside taxonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbarium_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herbarium-8">[8]</a></sup> For example, definition 6 is paired with the following definition of systematics that places nomenclature outside taxonomy:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henderson_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Henderson-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Systematics</i>: "The study of the identification, taxonomy, and nomenclature of organisms, including the classification of living things with regard to their natural relationships and the study of variation and the evolution of taxa".</li></ul>
<p>In 1970 Michener <i>et al.</i> defined "systematic biology" and "taxonomy" (terms that are often confused and used interchangeably) in relationship to one another as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Systematic biology (hereafter called simply systematics) is the field that (a) provides scientific names for organisms, (b) describes them, (c) preserves collections of them, (d) provides classifications for the organisms, keys for their identification, and data on their distributions, (e) investigates their evolutionary histories, and (f) considers their environmental adaptations. This is a field with a long history that in recent years has experienced a notable renaissance, principally with respect to theoretical content. Part of the theoretical material has to do with evolutionary areas (topics e and f above), the rest relates especially to the problem of classification. Taxonomy is that part of Systematics concerned with topics (a) to (d) above.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>A whole set of terms including taxonomy, systematic biology, systematics, biosystematics, scientific classification, biological classification, and <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetics</a> have at times had overlapping meanings – sometimes the same, sometimes slightly different, but always related and intersecting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilkins2011_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkins2011-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Small1989_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Small1989-10">[10]</a></sup> The broadest meaning of "taxonomy" is used here. The term itself was introduced in 1813 by <a href="/wiki/Augustin_Pyramus_de_Candolle" title="Augustin Pyramus de Candolle">de Candolle</a>, in his <i>Théorie élémentaire de la botanique</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Lindley" title="John Lindley">John Lindley</a> provided an early definition of systematics in 1830, although he wrote of "systematic botany" rather than using the term "systematics".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Europeans tend to use the terms "systematics" and "biosystematics" for the study of biodiversity as a whole, whereas North Americans tend to use "taxonomy" more frequently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> However, taxonomy, and in particular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha_taxonomy" title="Alpha taxonomy">alpha taxonomy</a>, is more specifically the identification, description, and naming (i.e. nomenclature) of organisms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fortey_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fortey-14">[14]</a></sup>
while "classification" focuses on placing organisms within hierarchical groups that show their relationships to other organisms.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monograph_and_taxonomic_revision">Monograph and taxonomic revision</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Monograph and taxonomic revision">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <b>taxonomic revision</b> or <b>taxonomic review</b> is a novel analysis of the variation patterns in a particular <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxon</a>. This analysis may be executed on the basis of any combination of the various available kinds of characters, such as morphological, anatomical, palynological, biochemical and genetic. A <a href="/wiki/Monograph#In_biology" title="Monograph">monograph</a> or complete revision is a revision that is comprehensive for a taxon for the information given at a particular time, and for the entire world. Other (partial) revisions may be restricted in the sense that they may only use some of the available character sets or have a limited spatial scope. A revision results in a conformation of or new insights in the relationships between the subtaxa within the taxon under study, which may lead to a change in the classification of these subtaxa, the identification of new subtaxa, or the merger of previous subtaxa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomic_characters">Taxonomic characters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Taxonomic characters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Taxonomic characters are the taxonomic attributes that can be used to provide the evidence from which relationships (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a>) between taxa  are inferred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Kinds of taxonomic characters include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 35em;">
<ul><li>Morphological characters
<ul><li>General external morphology</li>
<li>Special structures (e.g. genitalia)</li>
<li>Internal morphology (anatomy)</li>
<li>Embryology</li>
<li>Karyology and other cytological factors</li></ul></li>
<li>Physiological characters
<ul><li>Metabolic factors</li>
<li>Body secretions</li>
<li>Genic sterility factors</li></ul></li>
<li>Molecular characters
<ul><li>Immunological distance</li>
<li>Electrophoretic differences</li>
<li>Amino acid sequences of proteins</li>
<li>DNA hybridization</li>
<li>DNA and RNA sequences</li>
<li>Restriction endonuclease analyses</li>
<li>Other molecular differences</li></ul></li>
<li>Behavioral characters
<ul><li>Courtship and other ethological isolating mechanisms</li>
<li>Other behavior patterns</li></ul></li>
<li>Ecological characters
<ul><li>Habit and habitats</li>
<li>Food</li>
<li>Seasonal variations</li>
<li>Parasites and hosts</li></ul></li>
<li>Geographic characters
<ul><li>General biogeographic distribution patterns</li>
<li>Sympatric-allopatric relationship of populations</li></ul></li></ul>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alpha_and_beta_taxonomy">Alpha and beta taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Alpha and beta taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Alpha_diversity" title="Alpha diversity">Alpha diversity</a>.</div>
<p>The term "<b>alpha taxonomy</b>" is primarily used today to refer to the discipline of finding, describing, and naming <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxa</a>, particularly species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BiologyDiscussion_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-BiologyDiscussion-18">[18]</a></sup> In earlier literature, the term had a different meaning, referring to morphological taxonomy, and the products of research through the end of the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/William_Bertram_Turrill" title="William Bertram Turrill">William Bertram Turrill</a> introduced the term "alpha taxonomy" in a series of papers published in 1935 and 1937 in which he discussed the philosophy and possible future directions of the discipline of taxonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETurrill1938_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETurrill1938-20">[20]</a></sup></p><blockquote><p> ... there is an increasing desire amongst taxonomists to consider their problems from wider viewpoints, to investigate the possibilities of closer co-operation with their cytological, ecological and genetics colleagues and to acknowledge that some revision or expansion, perhaps of a drastic nature, of their aims and methods, may be desirable ... Turrill (1935) has suggested that while accepting the older invaluable taxonomy, based on structure, and conveniently designated "alpha", it is possible to glimpse a far-distant taxonomy built upon as wide a basis of morphological and physiological facts as possible, and one in which "place is found for all observational and experimental data relating, even if indirectly, to the constitution, subdivision, origin, and behaviour of species and other taxonomic groups". Ideals can, it may be said, never be completely realized. They have, however, a great value of acting as permanent stimulants, and if we have some, even vague, ideal of an "omega" taxonomy we may progress a little way down the Greek alphabet. Some of us please ourselves by thinking we are now groping in a "beta" taxonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETurrill1938_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETurrill1938-20">[20]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Turrill thus explicitly excludes from alpha taxonomy various areas of study that he includes within taxonomy as a whole, such as ecology, physiology, genetics, and cytology. He further excludes phylogenetic reconstruction from alpha taxonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETurrill1938365–366_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETurrill1938365–366-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Later authors have used the term in a different sense, to mean the delimitation of species (not subspecies or taxa of other ranks), using whatever investigative techniques are available, and including sophisticated computational or laboratory techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BiologyDiscussion_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-BiologyDiscussion-18">[18]</a></sup> Thus, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Mayr" title="Ernst Mayr">Ernst Mayr</a> in 1968 defined "<b>beta taxonomy</b>" as the classification of ranks higher than species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p><blockquote><p>An understanding of the biological meaning of variation and of the evolutionary origin of groups of related species is even more important for the second stage of taxonomic activity, the sorting of species into groups of relatives ("taxa") and their arrangement in a hierarchy of higher categories. This activity is what the term classification denotes; it is also referred to as "beta taxonomy".</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microtaxonomy_and_macrotaxonomy">Microtaxonomy and macrotaxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Microtaxonomy and macrotaxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Species_problem" title="Species problem">Species problem</a></div>
<p>How species should be defined in a particular group of organisms gives rise to practical and theoretical problems that are referred to as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Species_problem" title="Species problem">species problem</a>. The scientific work of deciding how to define species has been called microtaxonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BiologyDiscussion_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-BiologyDiscussion-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (April 2017)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup> By extension, macrotaxonomy is the study of groups at the higher <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_rank" title="Taxonomic rank">taxonomic ranks</a> subgenus and above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BiologyDiscussion_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-BiologyDiscussion-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>While some descriptions of taxonomic history attempt to date taxonomy to ancient civilizations, a truly scientific attempt to classify organisms did not occur until the 18th century. Earlier works were primarily descriptive and focused on plants that were useful in agriculture or medicine. There are a number of stages in this scientific thinking. Early taxonomy was based on arbitrary criteria, the so-called "artificial systems", including <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a>'s system of sexual classification for plants (Linnaeus's 1735 classification of animals was entitled "<a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a>" ("the System of Nature"), implying that he, at least, believed that it was more than an "artificial system"). Later came systems based on a more complete consideration of the characteristics of taxa, referred to as "natural systems", such as those of <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Laurent_de_Jussieu" title="Antoine Laurent de Jussieu">de Jussieu</a> (1789), de Candolle (1813) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bentham_and_Hooker" title="Bentham and Hooker">Bentham and Hooker</a> (1862–1863). These classifications described empirical patterns and were pre-<a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> in thinking. The publication of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Origin_of_Species" title="On the Origin of Species">On the Origin of Species</a></i> (1859) led to a new explanation for classifications, based on evolutionary relationships. This was the concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phyletic" title="Phyletic">phyletic</a> systems, from 1883 onwards. This approach was typified by those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/August_Eichler" title="August Eichler">Eichler</a> (1883) and <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Engler" title="Adolf Engler">Engler</a> (1886–1892). The advent of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cladistic" title="Cladistic">cladistic</a> methodology in the 1970s led to classifications based on the sole criterion of <a href="/wiki/Monophyly" title="Monophyly">monophyly</a>, supported by the presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synapomorphies" title="Synapomorphies">synapomorphies</a>. Since then, the evidentiary basis has been expanded with data from <a href="/wiki/Molecular_genetics" title="Molecular genetics">molecular genetics</a> that for the most part complements traditional <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDatta1988_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDatta1988-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2019)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStace1989_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStace1989-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2019)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStuessy2009_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStuessy2009-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2019)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Linnaean">Pre-Linnaean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Pre-Linnaean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_taxonomists">Early taxonomists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Early taxonomists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Naming and classifying human surroundings likely begun with the onset of language. Distinguishing poisonous plants from edible plants is integral to the survival of human communities. Medicinal plant illustrations show up in Egyptian wall paintings from c. 1500 BC, indicating that the uses of different species were understood and that a basic taxonomy was in place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manktelow_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manktelow-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_times">Ancient times</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ancient times">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_biology#Classification" title="Aristotle's biology">Aristotle's biology § Classification</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:372px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Huang-Quan-Xie-sheng-zhen-qin-tu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="6826" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Huang-Quan-Xie-sheng-zhen-qin-tu.jpg/370px-Huang-Quan-Xie-sheng-zhen-qin-tu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Huang-Quan-Xie-sheng-zhen-qin-tu.jpg/555px-Huang-Quan-Xie-sheng-zhen-qin-tu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Huang-Quan-Xie-sheng-zhen-qin-tu.jpg/740px-Huang-Quan-Xie-sheng-zhen-qin-tu.jpg 2x" width="370"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Huang-Quan-Xie-sheng-zhen-qin-tu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Description of rare animals</i> (写生珍禽图), by <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> painter <a href="/wiki/Huang_Quan_(painter)" title="Huang Quan (painter)">Huang Quan</a> (903–965)</div></div></div>
<p>Organisms were first classified by <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (<a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, 384–322 BC) during his stay on the <a href="/wiki/Lesbos" title="Lesbos">Island of Lesbos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palaeos_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Palaeos-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_–_Taxonomy_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_–_Taxonomy-32">[32]</a></sup> He classified beings by their parts, or in modern terms <i>attributes</i>, such as having live birth, having four legs, laying eggs, having blood, or being warm-bodied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roanoke_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roanoke-33">[33]</a></sup> He divided all living things into two groups: plants and animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palaeos_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Palaeos-31">[31]</a></sup> Some of his groups of animals, such as <i>Anhaima</i> (animals without blood, translated as <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a>) and <i>Enhaima</i> (animals with blood, roughly the <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>), as well as groups like the <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetacean" title="Cetacean">cetaceans</a>, are still commonly used today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> His student <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a> (Greece, 370–285 BC) carried on this tradition, mentioning some 500 plants and their uses in his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historia_Plantarum_(Theophrastus)" title="Historia Plantarum (Theophrastus)">Historia Plantarum</a></i>. Again, several plant groups currently still recognized can be traced back to Theophrastus, such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cornus_(genus)" title="Cornus (genus)">Cornus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Crocus" title="Crocus">Crocus</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Narcissus_(plant)" title="Narcissus (plant)">Narcissus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palaeos_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Palaeos-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval">Medieval</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Medieval">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Taxonomy in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> was largely based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristotelian_system" title="Aristotelian system">Aristotelian system</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roanoke_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roanoke-33">[33]</a></sup> with additions concerning the philosophical and existential order of creatures. This included concepts such as the <a href="/wiki/Great_chain_of_being" title="Great chain of being">great chain of being</a> in the Western <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">scholastic</a> tradition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roanoke_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Roanoke-33">[33]</a></sup> again deriving ultimately from Aristotle. The Aristotelian system did not classify plants or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>, due to the lack of microscopes at the time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_–_Taxonomy_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_–_Taxonomy-32">[32]</a></sup> as his ideas were based on arranging the complete world in a single continuum, as per the <i>scala naturae</i> (the Natural Ladder).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palaeos_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-Palaeos-31">[31]</a></sup> This, as well, was taken into consideration in the great chain of being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palaeos_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-Palaeos-31">[31]</a></sup> Advances were made by scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Procopius" title="Procopius">Procopius</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timotheos_of_Gaza" title="Timotheos of Gaza">Timotheos of Gaza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demetrios_Pepagomenos" title="Demetrios Pepagomenos">Demetrios Pepagomenos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>. Medieval thinkers used abstract philosophical and logical categorizations more suited to abstract philosophy than to pragmatic taxonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palaeos_31-5"><a href="#cite_note-Palaeos-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Renaissance_and_early_modern">Renaissance and early modern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Renaissance and early modern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>, categorizing organisms became more prevalent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palaeos_31-6"><a href="#cite_note-Palaeos-31">[31]</a></sup>
and taxonomic works became ambitious enough to replace the ancient texts. This is sometimes credited to the development of sophisticated optical lenses, which allowed the morphology of organisms to be studied in much greater detail. One of the earliest authors to take advantage of this leap in technology was the Italian physician <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Cesalpino" title="Andrea Cesalpino">Andrea Cesalpino</a> (1519–1603), who has been called "the first taxonomist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Masterpiece" title="Masterpiece">magnum opus</a> <i>De Plantis</i> came out in 1583, and described more than 1500 plant species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Two large plant families that he first recognized are still in use today: the <a href="/wiki/Asteraceae" title="Asteraceae">Asteraceae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brassicaceae" title="Brassicaceae">Brassicaceae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Then in the 17th century <a href="/wiki/John_Ray" title="John Ray">John Ray</a> (<a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, 1627–1705) wrote many important taxonomic works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_–_Taxonomy_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_–_Taxonomy-32">[32]</a></sup> Arguably his greatest accomplishment was <i>Methodus Plantarum Nova</i> (1682),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> in which he published details of over 18,000 plant species. At the time, his classifications were perhaps the most complex yet produced by any taxonomist, as he based his taxa on many combined characters. The next major taxonomic works were produced by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Pitton_de_Tournefort" title="Joseph Pitton de Tournefort">Joseph Pitton de Tournefort</a> (France, 1656–1708).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> His work from 1700, <i>Institutiones Rei Herbariae</i>, included more than 9000 species in 698 genera, which directly influenced Linnaeus, as it was the text he used as a young student.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manktelow_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Manktelow-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Linnaean_era">Linnaean era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Linnaean era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Linnaean_taxonomy" title="Linnaean taxonomy">Linnaean taxonomy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linn%C3%A9-Systema_Naturae_1735.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1141" data-file-width="910" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Linn%C3%A9-Systema_Naturae_1735.jpg/170px-Linn%C3%A9-Systema_Naturae_1735.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Linn%C3%A9-Systema_Naturae_1735.jpg/255px-Linn%C3%A9-Systema_Naturae_1735.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Linn%C3%A9-Systema_Naturae_1735.jpg/340px-Linn%C3%A9-Systema_Naturae_1735.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linn%C3%A9-Systema_Naturae_1735.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Title page of <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i>, Leiden, 1735</div></div></div>
<p>The Swedish botanist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> (1707–1778)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roanoke_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-Roanoke-33">[33]</a></sup> ushered in a new era of taxonomy. With his major works <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i> 1st Edition in 1735,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Species_Plantarum" title="Species Plantarum">Species Plantarum</a></i> in 1753,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae"><i>Systema Naturae</i> 10th Edition</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> he revolutionized modern taxonomy. His works implemented a standardized binomial naming system for animal and plant species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_–_Linnaean_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_–_Linnaean-44">[44]</a></sup> which proved to be an elegant solution to a chaotic and disorganized taxonomic literature. He not only introduced the standard of class, order, genus, and species, but also made it possible to identify plants and animals from his book, by using the smaller parts of the flower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_–_Linnaean_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_–_Linnaean-44">[44]</a></sup> Thus the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linnaean_system" title="Linnaean system">Linnaean system</a> was born, and is still used in essentially the same way today as it was in the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_–_Linnaean_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_–_Linnaean-44">[44]</a></sup> Currently, plant and animal taxonomists regard Linnaeus' work as the "starting point" for valid names (at 1753 and 1758 respectively).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Names published before these dates are referred to as "pre-Linnaean", and not considered valid (with the exception of spiders published in <i><a href="/wiki/Svenska_Spindlar" title="Svenska Spindlar">Svenska Spindlar</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>). Even taxonomic names published by Linnaeus himself before these dates are considered pre-Linnaean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manktelow_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Manktelow-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_system_of_classification">Modern system of classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Modern system of classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_taxonomy" title="Evolutionary taxonomy">Evolutionary taxonomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_nomenclature" title="Phylogenetic nomenclature">Phylogenetic nomenclature</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spindle_diagram.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="509" data-file-width="734" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Spindle_diagram.jpg/280px-Spindle_diagram.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Spindle_diagram.jpg/420px-Spindle_diagram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Spindle_diagram.jpg/560px-Spindle_diagram.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spindle_diagram.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Evolution of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrates" title="Vertebrates">vertebrates</a> at class level, width of spindles indicating number of families. Spindle diagrams are typical for <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_taxonomy" title="Evolutionary taxonomy">evolutionary taxonomy</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cladogram_vertebrata.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="337" data-file-width="734" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Cladogram_vertebrata.jpg/280px-Cladogram_vertebrata.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Cladogram_vertebrata.jpg/420px-Cladogram_vertebrata.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Cladogram_vertebrata.jpg/560px-Cladogram_vertebrata.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cladogram_vertebrata.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The same relationship, expressed as a <a href="/wiki/Cladogram" title="Cladogram">cladogram</a> typical for <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">cladistics</a></div></div></div>
<p>A pattern of groups nested within groups was specified by Linnaeus' classifications of plants and animals, and these patterns began to be represented as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dendrograms" title="Dendrograms">dendrograms</a> of the animal and plant <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" title="Kingdom (biology)">kingdoms</a> toward the end of the 18th century, well before Charles Darwin's <i>On the Origin of Species</i> was published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_–_Taxonomy_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_–_Taxonomy-32">[32]</a></sup> The pattern of the "Natural System" did not entail a generating process, such as evolution, but may have implied it, inspiring early transmutationist thinkers. Among early works exploring the idea of a <a href="/wiki/Transmutation_of_species" title="Transmutation of species">transmutation of species</a> were <a href="/wiki/Erasmus_Darwin" title="Erasmus Darwin">Erasmus Darwin</a>'s (Charles Darwin's grandfather's) 1796 <i><a href="/wiki/Zoonomia" title="Zoonomia">Zoönomia</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lamarck" title="Jean-Baptiste Lamarck">Jean-Baptiste Lamarck</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophie_Zoologique" title="Philosophie Zoologique">Philosophie Zoologique</a></i> of 1809.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BiologyDiscussion_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-BiologyDiscussion-18">[18]</a></sup> The idea was popularized in the Anglophone world by the speculative but widely read <i><a href="/wiki/Vestiges_of_the_Natural_History_of_Creation" title="Vestiges of the Natural History of Creation">Vestiges of the Natural History of Creation</a></i>, published anonymously by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Chambers_(publisher_born_1802)" title="Robert Chambers (publisher born 1802)">Robert Chambers</a> in 1844.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>With Darwin's theory, a general acceptance quickly appeared that a classification should reflect the Darwinian principle of <a href="/wiki/Common_descent" title="Common descent">common descent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_–_since_Linnaeus_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_–_since_Linnaeus-48">[48]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tree_of_life_(science)" title="Tree of life (science)">Tree of life</a> representations became popular in scientific works, with known fossil groups incorporated. One of the first modern groups tied to fossil ancestors was birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (May 2018)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Using the then newly discovered fossils of <i><a href="/wiki/Archaeopteryx" title="Archaeopteryx">Archaeopteryx</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hesperornis" title="Hesperornis">Hesperornis</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Huxley" title="Thomas Henry Huxley">Thomas Henry Huxley</a> pronounced that they had evolved from dinosaurs, a group formally named by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Owen" title="Richard Owen">Richard Owen</a> in 1842.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The resulting description, that of dinosaurs "giving rise to" or being "the ancestors of" birds, is the essential hallmark of <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_taxonomy" title="Evolutionary taxonomy">evolutionary taxonomic</a> thinking. As more and more fossil groups were found and recognized in the late 19th and early 20th centuries, <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">palaeontologists</a> worked to understand the history of animals through the ages by linking together known groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> With the <a href="/wiki/Modern_synthesis_(20th_century)" title="Modern synthesis (20th century)">modern evolutionary synthesis</a> of the early 1940s, an essentially modern understanding of the evolution of the major groups was in place. As evolutionary taxonomy is based on Linnaean taxonomic ranks, the two terms are largely interchangeable in modern use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cladistic" title="Cladistic">cladistic</a> method has emerged since the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_–_since_Linnaeus_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_–_since_Linnaeus-48">[48]</a></sup> In 1958, <a href="/wiki/Julian_Huxley" title="Julian Huxley">Julian Huxley</a> used the term <i>clade</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BiologyDiscussion_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-BiologyDiscussion-18">[18]</a></sup> Later, in 1960, Cain and Harrison introduced the term <i>cladistic</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BiologyDiscussion_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-BiologyDiscussion-18">[18]</a></sup> The salient feature is arranging taxa in a hierarchical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolutionary_tree" title="Evolutionary tree">evolutionary tree</a>, with the desideratum that all named taxa are monophyletic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_–_since_Linnaeus_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_–_since_Linnaeus-48">[48]</a></sup> A taxon is called monophyletic if it includes all the descendants of an ancestral form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mike_Taylor_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mike_Taylor-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCSE_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCSE-55">[55]</a></sup> Groups that have descendant groups removed from them are termed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paraphyletic" title="Paraphyletic">paraphyletic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mike_Taylor_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mike_Taylor-54">[54]</a></sup> while groups representing more than one branch from the tree of life are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyphyletic" title="Polyphyletic">polyphyletic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mike_Taylor_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mike_Taylor-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCSE_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-NCSE-55">[55]</a></sup> Monophyletic groups are recognized and diagnosed on the basis of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synapomorphies" title="Synapomorphies">synapomorphies</a>, shared derived character states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cladistic classifications are compatible with traditional Linnean taxonomy and the Codes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoological_nomenclature" title="Zoological nomenclature">Zoological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Botanical_nomenclature" title="Botanical nomenclature">Botanical nomenclature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> An alternative system of nomenclature, the <i><a href="/wiki/PhyloCode" title="PhyloCode">International Code of Phylogenetic Nomenclature</a></i> or <i>PhyloCode</i> has been proposed, whose intent is to regulate the formal naming of clades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phylocode_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phylocode-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (April 2017)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup> Linnaean ranks will be optional under the <i>PhyloCode</i>, which is intended to coexist with the current, rank-based codes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phylocode_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phylocode-59">[59]</a></sup> It remains to be seen whether the systematic community will adopt the <i>PhyloCode</i> or reject it in favor of the current systems of nomenclature that have been employed (and modified as needed) for over 250 years.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdoms_and_domains">Kingdoms and domains</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Kingdoms and domains">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="230" decoding="async" height="436" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg/170px-Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg/255px-Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg/340px-Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The basic scheme of modern classification. Many other levels can be used; domain, the highest level within life, is both new and disputed.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" title="Kingdom (biology)">Kingdom (biology)</a></div>
<p>Well before discovery of Carl Linnaeus (Botanist) plants and animals were considered separate Kingdoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdom_classification_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdom_classification-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (April 2017)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup> Linnaeus used this as the top rank, dividing the physical world into the vegetable, animal and mineral kingdoms. As advances in microscopy made classification of microorganisms possible, the number of kingdoms increased, five- and six-kingdom systems being the most common.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Domain_(biology)" title="Domain (biology)">Domains</a> are a relatively new grouping. First proposed in 1977, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Woese" title="Carl Woese">Carl Woese</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Three-domain_system" title="Three-domain system">three-domain system</a> was not generally accepted until later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> One main characteristic of the three-domain method is the separation of <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a>, previously grouped into the single kingdom Bacteria (a kingdom also sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Monera" title="Monera">Monera</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdom_classification_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdom_classification-60">[60]</a></sup> with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryota" title="Eukaryota">Eukaryota</a> for all organisms whose cells contain a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleus_(biology)" title="Nucleus (biology)">nucleus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> A small number of scientists include a sixth kingdom, Archaea, but do not accept the domain method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdom_classification_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdom_classification-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cavalier-Smith" title="Thomas Cavalier-Smith">Thomas Cavalier-Smith</a>, who published extensively on the classification of <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a>, recently<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (May 2021)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> proposed that the <a href="/wiki/Neomura" title="Neomura">Neomura</a>, the clade that groups together the Archaea and <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">Eucarya</a>, would have evolved from Bacteria, more precisely from <a href="/wiki/Actinomycetota" title="Actinomycetota">Actinomycetota</a>. His 2004 classification treated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeobacteria" title="Archaeobacteria">archaeobacteria</a> as part of a subkingdom of the kingdom Bacteria, i.e., he rejected the three-domain system entirely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CavalierSmith2004_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-CavalierSmith2004-63">[63]</a></sup> Stefan Luketa in 2012 proposed a five "dominion" system, adding <a href="/wiki/Prion" title="Prion">Prionobiota</a> (<a href="/wiki/Non-cellular_life" title="Non-cellular life">acellular</a> and without <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acid</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">Virusobiota</a> (acellular but <i>with</i> nucleic acid) to the traditional three domains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luketa2012_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luketa2012-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_comprehensive_classifications">Recent comprehensive classifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Recent comprehensive classifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Partial classifications exist for many individual groups of organisms and are revised and replaced as new information becomes available; however, comprehensive, published treatments of most or all life are rarer; recent examples are that of Adl et al., 2012 and 2019,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adl-et-al-2012_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adl-et-al-2012-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adl-et-al-2019_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adl-et-al-2019-73">[73]</a></sup> which covers eukaryotes only with an emphasis on protists, and Ruggiero et al., 2015,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruggiero-et-al-2015_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruggiero-et-al-2015-74">[74]</a></sup> covering both eukaryotes and <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a> to the rank of Order, although both exclude fossil representatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruggiero-et-al-2015_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ruggiero-et-al-2015-74">[74]</a></sup> A separate compilation (Ruggiero, 2014)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> covers extant taxa to the rank of Family. Other, database-driven treatments include the <a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_of_Life" title="Encyclopedia of Life">Encyclopedia of Life</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Global_Biodiversity_Information_Facility" title="Global Biodiversity Information Facility">Global Biodiversity Information Facility</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NCBI_taxonomy_database" title="NCBI taxonomy database">NCBI taxonomy database</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Interim_Register_of_Marine_and_Nonmarine_Genera" title="Interim Register of Marine and Nonmarine Genera">Interim Register of Marine and Nonmarine Genera</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Open_Tree_of_Life" title="Open Tree of Life">Open Tree of Life</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Catalogue_of_Life" title="Catalogue of Life">Catalogue of Life</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Paleobiology_Database" title="Paleobiology Database">Paleobiology Database</a> is a resource for fossils.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Application">Application</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Application">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Biological taxonomy is a sub-discipline of <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, and is generally practiced by biologists known as "taxonomists", though enthusiastic <a href="/wiki/Natural_history" title="Natural history">naturalists</a> are also frequently involved in the publication of new taxa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Because taxonomy aims to describe and organize <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>, the work conducted by taxonomists is essential for the study of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> and the resulting field of <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservation biology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classifying_organisms">Classifying organisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Classifying organisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_rank" title="Taxonomic rank">Taxonomic rank</a></div>
<p>Biological classification is a critical component of the taxonomic process. As a result, it informs the user as to what the relatives of the taxon are hypothesized to be. Biological classification uses taxonomic ranks, including among others (in order from most inclusive to least inclusive): <a href="/wiki/Domain_(biology)" title="Domain (biology)">Domain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" title="Kingdom (biology)">Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">Phylum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">Class</a>, <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">Order</a>, <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">Family</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">Genus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">Species</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Strain_(biology)" title="Strain (biology)">Strain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[note 1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomic_descriptions">Taxonomic descriptions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Taxonomic descriptions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Species_description" title="Species description">Species description</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nepenthes_smilesii_type_specimen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Nepenthes_smilesii_type_specimen.jpg/170px-Nepenthes_smilesii_type_specimen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Nepenthes_smilesii_type_specimen.jpg/255px-Nepenthes_smilesii_type_specimen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Nepenthes_smilesii_type_specimen.jpg/340px-Nepenthes_smilesii_type_specimen.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nepenthes_smilesii_type_specimen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Type_(biology)" title="Type (biology)">Type</a> specimen for <i><a href="/wiki/Nepenthes_smilesii" title="Nepenthes smilesii">Nepenthes smilesii</a></i>, a tropical <a href="/wiki/Pitcher_plant" title="Pitcher plant">pitcher plant</a></div></div></div>
<p>The "definition" of a taxon is encapsulated by its description or its diagnosis or by both combined. There are no set rules governing the definition of taxa, but the naming and publication of new taxa is governed by sets of rules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbarium_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Herbarium-8">[8]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Nomenclature" title="Nomenclature">nomenclature</a> for the more commonly used ranks (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superfamily_(taxonomy)" title="Superfamily (taxonomy)">superfamily</a> to <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a>), is regulated by the <i><a href="/wiki/International_Code_of_Zoological_Nomenclature" title="International Code of Zoological Nomenclature">International Code of Zoological Nomenclature</a></i> (<i>ICZN Code</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> In the fields of <a href="/wiki/Phycology" title="Phycology">phycology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mycology" title="Mycology">mycology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Botany" title="Botany">botany</a>, the naming of taxa is governed by the <i><a href="/wiki/International_Code_of_Nomenclature_for_algae,_fungi,_and_plants" title="International Code of Nomenclature for algae, fungi, and plants">International Code of Nomenclature for algae, fungi, and plants</a></i> (<i>ICN</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>The initial description of a taxon involves five main requirements:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>The taxon must be given a name based on the 26 letters of the Latin alphabet (a <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">binomial</a> for new species, or uninomial for other ranks).</li>
<li>The name must be unique (i.e. not a <a href="/wiki/Homonym_(biology)" title="Homonym (biology)">homonym</a>).</li>
<li>The description must be based on at least one name-bearing <a href="/wiki/Type_species" title="Type species">type specimen</a>.</li>
<li>It should include statements about appropriate attributes either to describe (define) the taxon or to differentiate it from other taxa (the diagnosis, <i>ICZN Code</i>, Article 13.1.1, <i>ICN</i>, Article 38). Both codes deliberately separate defining the content of a taxon (its <a href="/wiki/Circumscription_(taxonomy)" title="Circumscription (taxonomy)">circumscription</a>) from defining its name.</li>
<li>These first four requirements must be published in a work that is obtainable in numerous identical copies, as a permanent scientific record.</li></ol>
<p>However, often much more information is included, like the geographic range of the taxon, ecological notes, chemistry, behavior, etc. How researchers arrive at their taxa varies: depending on the available data, and resources, methods vary from simple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantitative_property" title="Quantitative property">quantitative</a> or <a href="/wiki/Qualitative_property" title="Qualitative property">qualitative</a> comparisons of striking features, to elaborate computer analyses of large amounts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DNA_sequence" title="DNA sequence">DNA sequence</a> data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Author_citation">Author citation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Author citation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Author_citation_(botany)" title="Author citation (botany)">Author citation (botany)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Author_citation_(zoology)" title="Author citation (zoology)">Author citation (zoology)</a></div>
<p>An "authority" may be placed after a scientific name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJE_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-AJE-85">[84]</a></sup> The authority is the name of the scientist or scientists who first validly published the name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJE_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-AJE-85">[84]</a></sup> For example, in 1758 Linnaeus gave the <a href="/wiki/Asian_elephant" title="Asian elephant">Asian elephant</a> the scientific name <i>Elephas maximus</i>, so the name is sometimes written as "<i>Elephas maximus</i> Linnaeus, 1758".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> The names of authors are frequently abbreviated: the abbreviation <i>L.</i>, for <i>Linnaeus,</i> is commonly used. In botany, there is, in fact, a regulated list of standard abbreviations (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_botanists_by_author_abbreviation" title="List of botanists by author abbreviation">list of botanists by author abbreviation</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> The system for assigning authorities differs slightly between <a href="/wiki/Author_citation_(botany)" title="Author citation (botany)">botany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Author_citation_(zoology)" title="Author citation (zoology)">zoology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbarium_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Herbarium-8">[8]</a></sup> However, it is standard that if the genus of a species has been changed since the original description, the original authority's name is placed in parentheses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phenetics">Phenetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Phenetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phylogenetics.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Phylogenetics.svg/300px-Phylogenetics.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Phylogenetics.svg/450px-Phylogenetics.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Phylogenetics.svg/600px-Phylogenetics.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phylogenetics.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A comparison of phylogenetic and phenetic (character-based) concepts</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Phenetics" title="Phenetics">Phenetics</a></div>
<p>In phenetics, also known as taximetrics, or numerical taxonomy, organisms are classified based on overall similarity, regardless of their phylogeny or evolutionary relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BiologyDiscussion_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-BiologyDiscussion-18">[18]</a></sup> It results in a measure of hypergeometric "distance" between taxa. Phenetic methods have become relatively rare in modern times, largely superseded by <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">cladistic</a> analyses, as phenetic methods do not distinguish shared ancestral (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plesiomorph" title="Plesiomorph">plesiomorphic</a>) traits from shared derived (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apomorphic" title="Apomorphic">apomorphic</a>) traits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> However, certain phenetic methods, such as <a href="/wiki/Neighbor_joining" title="Neighbor joining">neighbor joining</a>, have persisted, as rapid estimators of relationship when more advanced methods (such as <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_inference" title="Bayesian inference">Bayesian inference</a>) are too computationally expensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Databases">Databases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taxonomy_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Databases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_database" title="Taxonomic database">Taxonomic database</a></div>
<p>Modern taxonomy uses <a href="/wiki/Database" title="Database">database</a> technologies to search and catalogue classifications and their documentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> While there is no commonly used database, there are comprehensive databases such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Catalogue_of_Life" title="Catalogue of Life">Catalogue of Life</a></i>, which attempts to list every documented species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> The catalogue listed 1.64 million species for all kingdoms as of April 2016, claiming coverage of more than three quarters of the estimated species known to modern science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-About_the_Catalogue_of_Life_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-About_the_Catalogue_of_Life-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
